## 2026-05-10 11:42 / 中文内容分发

### 基本信息
- 平台：即刻
- slug：mini-program-and-website-relationship
- 标题：官网和小程序怎么配合，才不会最后两边各做各的
- 拟发布标题/主题：别太早把入口全收进小程序
- 状态：半自动待老板发送
- 链接目标页：https://sphrag.com/zh/blog/mini-program-and-website-relationship

### 最终文案
最近做官网和小程序一起上的项目，我越来越警惕一种做法：什么都还没讲清，就先把入口全收进小程序。

第一次来的人，很多并不是马上要操作，而是在判断你到底做什么、靠不靠谱、值不值得继续看。这一步如果没有官网承接，小程序里那些按钮其实很难替你建立信任。

所以我现在更倾向让官网先把信息讲明白，再让小程序去接预约、下单、查询这种动作。两边不是谁替代谁，而是谁先把人留下来，谁再把动作走完。

原文：https://sphrag.com/zh/blog/mini-program-and-website-relationship

### 平台

### 来源文章
- slug：website-development-cost
- 标题：网站开发多少钱：真正影响预算的不是一句“做个官网”

### 发布内容
- 发布标题：回答问题《做一个苹果官方网站需要多少钱？》
- 问题标题：做一个苹果官方网站需要多少钱？
- 问题链接：https://www.zhihu.com/question/23800100
- 问题ID：23800100
- 是否带链接：是
- 链接目标页：https://sphrag.com/zh/blog/website-development-cost
- 发布链接：

### 完整回答正文原文
如果你说的是“做一个看起来像苹果官网那样完整、顺滑、细节到位的官网”，那它通常不是普通企业展示站的预算。苹果那类官网贵的地方，不是买个服务器，也不是把页面切出来，而是信息架构、视觉打磨、产品图与视频素材、前端动效、响应式适配、性能优化、多语言、内容管理和长期维护都得一起做好。只问“做一个首页多少钱”，和真的把整站做出来，中间差的是一整个量级。

按我接项目的经验，这类网站大致可以分三档看。第一档只是借苹果风格做一个比较干净的品牌展示站，页面不多、动效也克制，通常还能控制在几万元级。第二档是更完整的企业官网，要有产品页、案例页、FAQ、联系路径，移动端和加载体验也要认真做，这时预算往往会上到十万左右甚至更高。第三档如果你真想接近苹果官网那种细腻程度，比如大量定制动效、精细过渡、视频和 3D 素材、多语言、多终端一致性和持续迭代，那就不是“便宜做一个站”的逻辑了，二三十万往上都不奇怪，关键还是看范围，不是看一个页面长什么样。

服务器反而通常不是最贵的部分。大多数企业站前期用云服务器或托管平台就够了，重点是带宽、静态资源分发、图片视频压缩和缓存策略，不一定要自己买物理服务器。时间上，如果只是轻量品牌站，几周到一两个月能推进；如果是内容很多、页面层级深、还要反复打磨动效和素材，周期拉到两三个月甚至更久都正常。真正影响成本和周期的，永远是范围有没有收住，而不是“像苹果”这三个字本身。

所以我一般不建议一上来就问“做一个苹果官网多少钱”，更有效的问法是：你到底要做几个页面，需不需要后台和多语言，素材是谁准备，动效做到什么程度，上线后谁维护。把这些说清楚，预算才会接近真实。这个问题我之前专门写过一版更细的拆法，你如果正在评估官网预算，可以直接看这篇：https://sphrag.com/zh/blog/website-development-cost

### 初步结果

- 是否被限流或异常：截至 2026-05-11 10:34（Asia/Shanghai），本轮重新执行 `autocli doctor`，结果为 `Chrome/Chromium ✓`、`Daemon running ✗`、`Chrome extension connected ✗`；随后执行 `agent-browser open https://www.zhihu.com/` 与 `agent-browser --auto-connect get url`，均返回 `Daemon failed to start (socket: /tmp/ab/default.sock)`；继续执行 `autocli zhihu search '企业官网 首页 怎么做' --format json --limit 8` 与 `autocli zhihu search 'SaaS 官网 页面 结构' --format json --limit 8`，均返回 `Chrome is not running`。当前本地独立调度器没有可用的知乎自动发布链路，已按规则降级为半自动待老板手动发布。

## 2026-05-11 11:14 / 中文内容分发

### 平台
- 掘金

### 来源文章
- slug：website-internal-system-shared-auth
- 标题：官网和内部系统要不要共用账号体系？很多项目不是不能做，而是做得太早

### 发布内容
- 发布标题：官网和内部系统要不要共用一套账号？我一般先看这 4 个边界
- 问题标题：
- 是否带链接：是
- 链接目标页：https://sphrag.com/zh/blog/website-internal-system-shared-auth
- 发布链接：待老板手动发布
- 分类建议：后端（若现场无此分类，退回选“开发工具”）
- 标签建议：`系统设计`、`权限设计`、`SSO`
- 摘要建议：很多团队一做官网加后台，就想顺手把账号体系打通。但真实项目里，先失控的往往不是登录本身，而是身份类型、权限模型、首期范围和审计责任。我通常先看这 4 个边界，再决定要不要做统一账号。

### 最终掘金正文
很多团队一做官网加后台，就会顺手提出一个需求：既然两边都要登录，不如账号体系一次打通。

这个想法听起来很顺，但我现在做这类项目，通常不会先聊 SSO，也不会先聊用户表怎么合。因为真实项目里，先失控的往往不是登录本身，而是身份边界没拆清，结果把客户入口、员工入口、权限模型和审计责任一起做重了。

所以我现在判断官网和内部系统要不要共用账号，通常会先看下面这 4 个边界。

## 1. 先看是不是同一类用户，真的要跨两个入口连续操作

统一账号最有价值的情况，不是“两个系统都需要登录”，而是“同一个人真的要在两个入口之间连续办同一件事”。

比如客户先在官网了解方案，再进客户门户看报价、下订单、下载资料；或者合作伙伴先在官网看政策，再进入渠道系统处理权限和进度。这种场景里，身份连续性能明显减少摩擦。

但如果官网主要面对陌生访客和线索收集，内部系统主要给销售、运营、交付或财务使用，那通常就不是一类用户。这个时候强行做统一账号，看起来架构完整，实际上只是在提前引入一套更重的账户管理问题。

## 2. 共用登录入口，不等于共用一套权限模型

很多团队一说统一账号，就默认用户表、角色、组织关系、权限规则都能一起合并。这一步最容易把项目做歪。

客户账号更关心的是公司信息、联系人、订单和资料可见范围；员工账号更关心的是部门、岗位、审批链、操作留痕。两边即使都能登录，也不代表应该被塞进同一种权限语义里。

我更倾向把认证和业务权限拆成两层。认证层解决“你是谁、怎么登录、账号是否有效”；业务层再分别处理客户、员工、合作伙伴各自的权限边界。这样后面真要打通，也是在清楚边界上扩，而不是把所有身份揉成一个越来越难维护的大权限团。

## 3. 一期最怕把客户入口和员工入口一起做满

真实项目里，很多复杂度不是登录协议本身带来的，而是一期开太大。

官网想让客户注册，后台想让员工共用账号，管理层又想顺手加供应商或渠道商入口。结果注册、邀请、找回密码、账号禁用、组织归属切换、审计留痕、风控策略会一起冲进范围里，测试和运维压力立刻上来。

更麻烦的是，客户身份和员工身份的风险等级通常根本不同。外部用户更在意自助注册和重置体验，内部员工更在意权限审计、离职禁用和责任追溯。两边如果一期硬压在同一套方案里，最后往往谁都不满意。

## 4. 比起先问技术怎么做，我更建议先画身份边界图

如果团队确实在评估统一账号，我现在通常会先让大家把一张很朴素的图画出来：有哪些用户类型，各自从哪个入口进来，要做哪些动作，需要多强的验证，谁能邀请谁，谁能禁用谁，哪些行为必须审计，哪些系统只读身份，哪些系统会写角色和组织关系。

这张图一旦清楚，技术方案反而不难。最后的答案，可能是官网和客户门户共用一套外部身份，员工后台继续独立；也可能是认证源统一，但权限域分开。关键不是追求“统一登录”这个词本身，而是让身份设计服从真实业务边界。

很多项目不是不能做统一账号，而是做得太早、做得太满。比起一开始就追求所有入口打通，我更相信先把主用户群、权限责任和一期范围收清楚，再决定哪里值得真正共用。

原文延伸：https://sphrag.com/zh/blog/website-internal-system-shared-auth

### 初步结果
- 是否发布成功：否
- 是否被限流或异常：截至 2026-05-11 11:14（Asia/Shanghai），本轮重新执行 `autocli doctor`，结果为 `Chrome/Chromium ✓`、`Daemon running ✗`、`Chrome extension connected ✗`；随后执行 `autocli --help` 确认当前命令列表中没有 `juejin` 子命令；继续执行 `env HOME='/Users/junbinzhong/workplace/agent-dirs/openclaw-cron-migration/assets/openclaw/workspace-outsourcing' AGENT_BROWSER_HOME='/Users/junbinzhong/workplace/agent-dirs/openclaw-cron-migration/assets/openclaw/workspace-outsourcing/.agent-browser' AGENT_BROWSER_SOCKET_DIR='/tmp/ab-openclaw-socket' agent-browser --auto-connect open 'https://juejin.cn/editor/drafts/new?v=2'` 返回 `Daemon failed to start (socket: /tmp/ab-openclaw-socket/default.sock)`。当前本地独立调度器没有可用的掘金浏览器自动发布链路，无法可靠进入编辑器、判断是否为 CodeMirror、补齐分类/标签/摘要并完成发布，已按 SOP 降级为半自动待老板发布。
- 是否有点击：

## 2026-05-11 11:42 / 中文内容分发

### 基本信息
- 平台：即刻
- slug：landing-page-vs-company-website
- 标题：营销落地页和企业官网有什么区别，什么时候该做哪一种
- 拟发布标题/主题：先别急着把落地页做成小官网
- 状态：半自动待老板发送
- 链接目标页：https://sphrag.com/zh/blog/landing-page-vs-company-website

### 最终文案
最近我做落地页，最先问的一句已经不是“这页要放多少内容”，而是“这页到底想让人做哪一个动作”。

如果这一页既想讲公司、又想讲全部服务、又想放案例、还想顺手收咨询，最后通常就是看起来很满，目标却散了。用户不是不看，是看完还是不知道下一步该点哪里。

所以我现在更愿意把落地页当成只完成一件事的页面。要长期讲清品牌、服务和内容，还是交给官网。最怕的就是把落地页做成小官网，最后两边都不像。

原文：https://sphrag.com/zh/blog/landing-page-vs-company-website

- Canonical Link：https://sphrag.com/en/blog/website-first-or-system-first

### 半自动成稿
建议平台：Medium
选中的 slug：website-first-or-system-first
标题：Most Companies Should Not Build the Internal System First
副标题：If prospects still do not understand what you do, a new system will not solve the real bottleneck. If delivery is already breaking under manual coordination, another website refresh will not solve it either.

完整正文：

# Most Companies Should Not Build the Internal System First

When a company starts talking about a digital upgrade, one question appears very quickly: should we build the website first, or should we build the internal system first?

Many teams treat that as a technology preference question. In practice, I think it is usually a bottleneck diagnosis question.

The wrong move is to assume that the more complex project must be the more urgent one. A new internal system can feel like a stronger sign of progress, but it does not automatically solve the problem that is actually slowing the business down. If prospects still do not understand what the company does, what kind of projects it fits, or how to move forward, internal tooling will not suddenly create clarity. On the other hand, if demand already exists and the team is drowning in spreadsheets, chat handoffs, and repeated data entry, another round of website polish will not fix the daily operational cost.

The better starting point is to ask where the current friction is most expensive. Is the business still hard to understand from the outside, or is it already hard to operate from the inside? Once that answer is honest, the project order usually becomes much easier to judge.

## 1. Build the website first when the business is still difficult to understand

For many B2B companies, the website is not a cosmetic layer. It is the first working explanation of the business.

Before a prospect ever talks to sales, they are usually trying to answer very basic questions. What does this company actually do? What kinds of problems does it solve? What sort of client is it a fit for? What makes the service credible enough to continue the conversation? If those answers are still buried, fragmented, or inconsistent, the business is asking people to trust it before it has explained itself clearly.

That is why I usually recommend website-first when lead generation still depends too heavily on referrals, repeated manual explanation, or one person constantly translating the business for every new opportunity. In that stage, the website is doing real operational work. It reduces repeated clarification, sharpens service boundaries, and gives prospects a cleaner path into the conversation.

This does not mean “make a prettier homepage.” It means clarifying positioning, service scope, case presentation, FAQ, and contact flow so the business becomes easier to understand without a live walkthrough every time. If that layer is weak, building internal tooling first often improves a machine that still does not have enough clean demand entering it.

## 2. Build the system first when manual coordination is already the real tax

There is another kind of company where the website is not the main problem anymore.

The team already has demand. Deals are moving. The real pain starts after the work begins. Requirements are buried in message threads. Status updates depend on memory. The same information gets copied across different spreadsheets. People are unsure who owns the next step. Everyone stays busy, but the system around the work is still mostly manual.

In that situation, another website refresh usually creates only marginal value. The business is not suffering because the public explanation is weak. It is suffering because delivery, coordination, and traceability are weak.

That is where a system-first decision starts to make sense. But even here, I would still add one condition: the team should already have a visible enough workflow to structure. If the process itself is still chaotic, building software too early just hardens confusion into a more expensive shape. A good system project is not a substitute for operational thinking. It works best when the organization already knows which loop is worth standardizing and which repeated work is costing too much to keep handling manually.

## 3. Most teams do not need an absolute answer. They need sequencing

In real projects, the answer is often not “website or system forever.” It is “which one should lead this phase?”

Many companies eventually need both. They need a stronger public-facing explanation and a stronger internal operating layer. The real mistake is trying to do all of it at once because that sounds more complete.

When teams attempt to rebuild the website, launch a new internal system, and sometimes even add a mini-program in the same phase, they usually spread attention too thin. Each piece gets delivered, but not deeply enough to change the business meaningfully. The website still does not explain enough. The system still does not control the workflow well enough. The overall result looks ambitious on paper and shallow in practice.

I trust phased sequencing much more. If market clarity is the urgent issue, start with the website and add only the minimum backend support required for lead capture or content updates. If operational pressure is the urgent issue, start with one critical workflow in the system and keep the website good enough rather than overbuilt. That kind of narrower first phase usually creates better evidence for the second one.

## 4. The best decision usually comes from mapping where work gets stuck today

When I talk with teams about this choice, I do not start with feature lists. I start by asking where the business gets blocked.

Where do deals slow down? Where does explanation keep repeating? Where does delivery become fragile? Where does ownership become fuzzy? Which part of the work still depends too much on a few people carrying knowledge in chat threads or in their heads?

Those questions usually reveal much more than a high-level debate about “digital transformation.” They show whether the company needs external clarity first or internal control first. And they help avoid one of the most common mistakes in software planning: spending heavily on the layer that feels more advanced instead of the layer that is actually causing the loss.

That is why I do not think the website-versus-system decision should be framed as a prestige choice. It should be framed as a bottleneck decision. Solve the most expensive friction first, and the roadmap usually becomes much less ideological and much more practical.

If you are deciding whether the website or the internal system should come first, the original article with more detail is here: https://sphrag.com/en/blog/website-first-or-system-first

## 2026-05-13 11:14 / 中文内容分发

### 平台
- 掘金

### 来源文章
- slug：legacy-erp-refactor-database-api-permission
- 标题：老 ERP 想重构时，数据库、接口、权限三件事先动哪个更稳？

### 发布内容
- 发布标题：老 ERP 重构先动数据库、接口还是权限？我更建议这样排顺序
- 问题标题：
- 是否带链接：是
- 链接目标页：https://sphrag.com/zh/blog/legacy-erp-refactor-database-api-permission
- 发布链接：待老板手动发布
- 分类建议：后端（若现场无此分类，退回选“开发工具”）
- 标签建议：`系统设计`、`后端`、`企业系统`
- 摘要建议：老 ERP 重构最怕数据库、接口、权限三层一起动。多数情况下更该先收接口边界，只有数据口径已经失真时数据库才值得前置，权限则从第一阶段就要当成硬约束。

### 最终掘金正文
很多团队一提老 ERP 重构，第一句话就是“数据库太乱了，先把库重做”。

我现在反而会先踩一下刹车。因为真实项目里，数据库、接口、权限这三层如果一起开刀，往往还没解决最痛的问题，范围先失控了。最后看起来什么都在重构，实际业务事故、返工和维护成本并没有先降下来。

所以我现在判断顺序，通常不是看哪一层听起来更底层，而是先看哪一层正在持续制造业务问题。顺序判断对了，重构才像交付；顺序判断错了，很容易直接做成长周期消耗战。

## 1. 大多数情况下，先收接口边界，比先翻数据库更稳

只要老 ERP 还在跑，最现实的约束通常就不是“架构纯不纯”，而是业务不能停。

这个时候如果直接大改数据库，外围系统、报表、同步脚本、补数据习惯、历史接口基本都会被一起卷进来，影响面非常难控。很多项目不是技术做不动，而是一下子牵连太多调用点，现场很快没人敢继续推进。

我更常见的做法，是先把接口层收紧。先定义哪些动作允许外部调用，哪些字段可以写，哪些状态变化必须走明确流程，哪些高风险写操作必须留痕。先把写入口收住，很多隐藏问题才会真正浮出来。

接口层不一定最底，但它往往最适合先止血。因为只要入口开始可控，后面才看得清哪些逻辑该留，哪些字段其实早就不该被多个系统同时改。

## 2. 数据库只有在“数据真相已经烂掉”时，才值得前置

当然，也不是所有项目都该先动接口。

如果核心对象的数据口径已经失真，比如同一客户有多套主键、同一订单在几张表里状态对不上、库存占用和出入库关系已经混乱到接口层也解释不清，那这时候继续包接口，很多时候只是给脏地基再刷一层墙。

但就算数据库该前置，我也不建议一上来就喊“全库重做”。更稳的做法，通常是先围绕一条主链路重整核心模型，比如订单主表、明细、状态流转、库存占用这些最容易传导错误的部分。先把最关键的一小块地基打稳，比一开始就做全库大迁移更接近可交付。

换句话说，数据库该不该先动，不看它是不是更底层，而看它是不是已经坏到别的层补不住了。

## 3. 权限不一定先单独大修，但必须从第一阶段就当成硬约束

老 ERP 里还有一层经常被低估，就是权限。

很多系统表面上有角色表，实际关键动作只是靠前端隐藏按钮，或者默认给管理员万能权限。结果接口和数据刚整理一点，后面又会被越权操作重新打乱。你前面收住的边界，转头又被现场“先改一下再说”冲开了。

所以权限不一定要第一个单独做成一套大而全的改造工程，但它必须从第一阶段就进入方案。哪些角色能改关键状态，哪些字段必须审批后写入，哪些操作必须留痕，哪些人工修正需要原因和审计，这些都应该先卡住。

否则项目很容易变成：接口层看起来规范了，数据库局部也整理了，但因为权限边界还是虚的，最后谁都能绕过去改，系统很快又回到老样子。

## 4. 真正能落地的顺序，通常是先止血，再拆主链路，再补完整模型

如果一定要把顺序说得更落地一点，我通常会这样排：

先用接口层把高风险写操作收住，再判断核心数据模型是不是已经坏到必须优先重整，最后再把权限模型补成长期可维护的约束体系。

这套顺序看起来没有“全量重构”那么激进，但更符合真实交付。因为它先解决正在出事故的地方，再逐步扩大范围，而不是一开始就把数据库、接口、权限一起重做，然后让项目在中途失速。

老 ERP 重构最怕的，不是技术债多，而是三层一起动却没有主次。比起先争论哪层更高级，我更建议先把真实业务风险拆出来：到底是写入口失控、数据真相失真，还是权限边界太松。这个顺序一旦判断对，后面的每一步都会稳很多。

原文延伸：https://sphrag.com/zh/blog/legacy-erp-refactor-database-api-permission

- 状态：半自动待老板发送

### 最终文案
这两年做网站项目，我发现最容易把合作做累的一句话，不是“这里改一下”，而是“这个也算维护吧”。

因为 bug 修复、内容替换、样式微调、新增页面、流程改动，很多时候都会被顺手塞进“维护”两个字里。上线前不拆开，后面每一次修改都像在重新谈边界。

所以我现在更愿意一开始就把维护写具体：哪些算修复，哪些算日常更新，哪些已经是新需求。话说清楚，合作反而最省心。

原文：https://sphrag.com/zh/blog/website-maintenance-scope

标题：Most Internal Dashboards Should Not Start With Real-Time Queries
副标题：When I plan reporting for internal systems, I separate operational views from stable management metrics before I argue about chart speed or SQL optimization.

完整正文：

# Most Internal Dashboards Should Not Start With Real-Time Queries

One sentence shows up in internal-system projects again and again: “leadership wants real-time data.”

I usually slow the discussion down when I hear that. In my experience, this is rarely just a charting question or a database tuning question. It is usually a reporting-model question first.

The real problem is that different people mean different things by “real time.” Operators want to know what needs action right now. Managers want numbers that stay comparable across today, this week, and this month. Finance and audit teams want past checkpoints to remain explainable later. If we push all of those needs into one live-query path, the result is often the worst combination: slow dashboards and unstable numbers at the same time.

That is why I do not start internal reporting by asking whether everything should be real time. I start by asking which decisions each report is supposed to support.

## 1. Live queries are useful when someone needs to act now

I still use live queries, but I use them for the kind of reporting that supports immediate action.

If a support team needs to see unresolved tickets, if warehouse staff need the latest shipment queue, or if operations need to monitor active payment exceptions, freshness matters more than historical stability. In those cases, the screen is not a monthly truth source. It is an operational surface. A little caching is fine, but the basic job of the report is to help someone decide the next move right away.

Trouble starts when the exact same logic gets reused for executive summaries, department reviews, or daily management numbers. Transactional data gets corrected, backfilled, voided, and rewritten. Status definitions can move during the day. I have seen teams call this a data mismatch problem when it was really a decision-context problem. They were expecting settlement-grade numbers from operational-grade data.

## 2. Snapshot layers are not just performance tricks

When I suggest a snapshot table or an aggregation layer, some teams hear that as “the queries are too slow.”

Performance is part of it, but it is not the main reason. The bigger value is that a snapshot layer gives the business a stable checkpoint. Daily sales totals, weekly customer growth, monthly completed deliveries, or any number tied to management review becomes much easier to trust when there is a deliberate rule for when that fact is frozen, recomputed, or versioned.

This also protects the transaction path. Ordering, approvals, edits, and integrations already put enough pressure on a production system. If heavy grouping, cross-table aggregation, and trend analysis stay on the same path forever, reporting will keep competing with the operational workload. I have seen internal dashboards become a system problem not because the charts were too ambitious, but because nobody admitted early enough that transaction processing and management analysis are different loads.

## 3. Phase one should be split by decision cadence, not by a real-time versus offline slogan

The most practical reporting plans I have worked on did not start with a binary choice. They started by splitting reports into three groups.

First, operational reporting: near-real-time views that help people act on current work. Second, management reporting: hourly or daily summaries that prioritize stable definitions and comparable trends. Third, audit or finance reporting: outputs that need traceability, checkpoint rules, and sometimes explicit settlement snapshots.

Once I frame the problem that way, the architecture conversation becomes much cleaner. We stop arguing about whether the whole system is real time and start deciding which refresh rhythm each decision deserves. That also keeps phase one under control. Too many teams try to make every list, every dashboard, and every reconciliation view share one reporting path. Then they ask for second-level freshness, unchanged history, and fast queries all at once. That combination usually collapses under its own expectations.

## 4. Metric ownership matters more than the chart library

The reporting failures I see most often are not caused by ECharts, React, or SQL alone.

They usually come from vague metric ownership. Who defines a closed deal? Who decides when an order counts as completed? Who owns the meaning of a valid customer, received payment, or approved exception? If those answers are fuzzy, one team adds a frontend filter, another changes a backend state mapping, and a third adds a correction rule later. Everyone may be technically reasonable, but the report still has no stable explanation.

That is why I now begin reporting projects with a short set of questions: who uses this report to make what decision, how much delay is acceptable, whether history must freeze, whether later corrections should rewrite the past, and who owns the final metric definition. Once those answers exist, choosing live queries, caching, scheduled summaries, or snapshot batches becomes much less ideological and much more practical.

If you are planning internal reporting and debating live queries versus snapshots, the original article is here: https://sphrag.com/en/blog/internal-reporting-realtime-vs-snapshot
